Dispute on Consideration of Time: Shooting vs Injury in Game of Haram
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for game in Haram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between arrow shooting and dog release regarding liability for game entering Haram, and identify the role of istihsan in overriding qiyas.

Core Idea
Arrow shooter is liable because no free agent intervenes; dog releaser is not because the dog's free will breaks causation.

The Hanafi school generally considers the time of shooting for liability. However, when game is shot from Halal and struck in Haram, there is a dispute: some reports impose penalty by istihsan while others follow the general principle. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the exception and how it differs from releasing a dog.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
State the problem: person shoots from Halal, arrow hits game in Haram. Is he liable?
2
State the general Hanafi principle
According to Abu Hanifa, the time of shooting is the basis for liability, not the time of injury.
3
Note the exception and its basis
Muhammad reports that Abu Hanifa made an exception for this specific case via istihsan, because the arrow's effect is direct with no intervening free agent.
Contrast with the dog case where the dog's free will breaks attribution.
4
Analyze the distinction between arrow and dog
The arrow's injury is attributed to the shooter as long as no free agent intervenes; the dog's action is its own, so the releaser is not liable.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If liable, penalty is due; if not, no penalty. In both cases, the game is not eaten.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهل المعتبر حالة الرامي أو الإصابة؟ ففي فتاوى قاضيخان: لو رمى صيداً في الحل فنفر الصيد ووقع السهم في الحرم قال محمد عليه الجزاء في قول أبي حنيفة فيما أعلم اهـ. وذكر في المبسوط مثله في آخر المناسك، وذكر في موضع آخر أنه لا يلزمه الجزاء لأنه في الرمي غير مرتكب للنهي ولكن لا يحل تناول ذلك الصيد، وهذه المسألة المستثناة من أصل أبي حنيفة فإن عنده المعتبر حالة الرمي إلا في هذه المسألة خاصة فإنه يعتبر في حل التناول حالة الإصابة احتياطاً لأن الحل يحصل بالذكاة، وإنما يكون ذلك عند الإصابة وعلى هذا إرسال الكلب ا هـ. وقد اختلف كلامه لكن ذكر في البدائع أنه لا جزاء. قال الحموي: هذا عجيب منه فقد صرح به في متن النقاية حيث قال: وكذا ذبح الحلال صيد الحرم أو حلبه. قال الشراح: أي حلب الصيد فإنه يجب عليه قيمة اللبن ا هـ. قلت: وكذا في متن الملتقى. قوله: (فإنه يعتبر في حل التناول حالة الإصابة) تقييده بحل التناول يقتضي أن الاستثناء المذكور بالنسبة إليه لا بالنسبة إلى وجوب الجزاء وعدمه. أن عبارة البدائع مصرح: مع الجزاء استحسان، وسيذكر المؤلف التوفيق بالحمل على الاستحسان فيكون الاستثناء مبنياً على الاستحسان وهو وجوب الجزاء لا حل التناول فتدبر. وعبارة البدائع هكذا: ولو أرسل كلباً في الحل على صيد في الحل فاتبعه الكلب فأخذه في الحرم فقتله لا شيء على المرسل، ولا يؤكل الصيد، أما الأول فلأن العبرة في وجوب الضمان لحالة الإرسال إذ هو السبب للضمان والإرسال وقع مباحاً فلا يتعلق به الضمان، وأما الثاني فلأن فعل الكلب ذبح للصيد وأنه حصل في الحرم فلا يحل أكله كما لو ذبحه آدمي إذ فعل الكلب لا يكون أعلى من فعل الآدمي. ولو رمى صيداً في الحل فنفر الصيد فوقع السهم به في الحرم فعليه الجزاء قال محمد في الأصل: وهو قول أبي حنيفة فيما أعلم، وكان القياس أن لا يجب عليه الجزاء كما في إرسال الكلب وخاصة على أصل أبي حنيفة فإنه يعتبر حالة الرمي في المسائل حتى قال فيمن رمي إلى مسلم فارتد المرمى إليه ثم أصابه السهم فقتله إنه يجب عليه الدية اعتباراً بحالة الرمي. إلا أنهم استحسنوا فأوجبوا الجزاء في الرمي دون الإرسال لأن الرمي مؤثر في الإصابة بمجرى العادة إن لم يتخلل بين الرمي والإصابة فعل فاعل مختار يقطع نسبة الأثر إليه شرعاً فبقيت الإصابة مضافة إليه شرعاً في الأحكام فصار كأنه ابتدأ الرمي بعد ما حصل الصيد في الحرم، وقد تخلل بين الإرسال والأخذ فعل فاعل مختار وهو الكلب فمنع إضافة الأخذ إلى المرسل. انتهى ملخصاً.
And is consideration the state of the shooter or the injury? In Fatawa Qadikhan: If one shoots at game in the Halal, and the game flees and the arrow strikes in the Haram, Muhammad said: he owes the penalty, according to Abu Hanifa as far as I know. And it is mentioned in al-Mabsut similarly at the end of al-Manasik, and in another place that he is not liable for the penalty because in shooting he is not violating the prohibition, but it is not permissible to eat that game. This issue is an exception from the principle of Abu Hanifa, for according to him consideration is the state of shooting, except in this particular issue, for he considers the state of injury regarding the permissibility of consuming, as a precaution, because permissibility is achieved by slaughter, and that only occurs at the time of injury. And upon this is the release of the dog. His statement is disputed, but it is mentioned in al-Bada'i' that there is no penalty. Al-Hamawi said: This is strange from him, for it is explicitly stated in the text of al-Niqayah where he said: Likewise, a Halal who slaughters game of the Haram or milks it. The commentators said: i.e., milking the game, for he must pay the value of the milk. I say: Likewise in the text of al-Multaqa. His statement: 'He considers the time of injury regarding the permissibility of consumption' — his restriction to 'permissibility of consumption' implies that the exception mentioned pertains to it, not to the existence or non-existence of the penalty. The expression of al-Bada'i' explicitly states: 'with the penalty it is istihsan.' And the author will mention the reconciliation by attributing it to istihsan, so the exception is based on istihsan, which is the obligation of the penalty, not the permissibility of consumption. So reflect. And the expression of al-Bada'i' is as follows: If one releases a dog in the Halal at game in the Halal, and the dog follows and catches it in the Haram and kills it, nothing is upon the releaser, and the game is not eaten. As for the first, because consideration in liability is the state of release, for it is the cause of liability, and the release occurred permissible, so liability does not attach. As for the second, because the act of the dog is a slaughter of the game, and it occurred in the Haram, so it is not permissible to eat it, just as if a human had slaughtered it, for the dog's act is not higher than the human's act. And if one shoots at game in the Halal, and the game flees and the arrow strikes it in the Haram, he owes the penalty. Muhammad said in al-Asl: This is the statement of Abu Hanifa as far as I know. The analogy would be that he should not owe the penalty, as in the release of the dog, especially according to Abu Hanifa's principle that he considers the state of shooting in issues, as he said regarding one who shoots at a Muslim and the targeted person apostatizes and then the arrow kills him: he must pay the blood-money, considering the state of shooting. However, they applied istihsan and imposed the penalty in shooting but not in release, because shooting is effective in causing injury by normal course, unless an act of a free agent intervenes between the shot and injury that cuts the attribution of the effect to him legally. So the injury remains attributed to him legally in the rulings, so it is as if he began the shooting after the game had entered the Haram. In release, an act of a free agent, the dog, intervenes, so the attribution of the catching to the releaser is prevented. End of summary.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage addresses a nuanced question: when a person shoots an arrow from the Halal and the animal moves into the Haram before being hit, is the person liable? The Hanafi position is that according to Abu Hanifa, generally the time of shooting is considered. However, this specific case is an exception: he considers the time of injury regarding the permissibility of eating, but for liability, the majority view (including Abu Hanifa according to Muhammad) is that the penalty is owed as an istihsan. The reasoning is that the shooter's act is the direct cause of death if no free agent intervenes. In contrast, if a dog is sent, the dog's free choice intervenes, so the penalty is not on the sender. This distinction is based on the principle that the arrow's effect is direct, while the dog's action is attributed to the dog itself. The text also notes a difference in the sources: some say no penalty, but the relied-upon view is that penalty is owed by istihsan.

Key Terms
الرمي (al-ramy): Shooting an arrow; the act of the hunter.
الإرسال (al-irsal): Releasing a dog or falcon to chase game.
الاستحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; overriding qiyas for a more suitable ruling.
حل التناول (hall al-tanawul): Permissibility of consuming the game.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a shooter who kills game in the Haram with an arrow shot from the Halal liable, while a person who releases a dog that kills game in the Haram is not?
A) Because the arrow is a weapon, while the dog is a tool.
B) Because the shooter intended to kill, while the dog's owner did not.
C) Because the arrow's effect is direct without intervening free agent, unlike the dog.
D) Because the dog is trained, while the arrow is not.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between arrow shooting and dog release based on whether a free agent intervenes, with istihsan imposing liability on the shooter but not the releaser.

Game Partially in the Haram: The Hanafi Ruling on Penalty (Jaza')
Ruling on hunting game partially inside the Haram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of taghlib al-khatar 'ala al-ibaha to determine when a muhrim incurs a penalty for killing game partially inside the Haram.

Core Idea
Any part of the game in the Haram triggers the penalty—sanctity predominates over permissibility.

The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a common question: if a game animal is only partially inside the sacred precinct (Haram), does the muhrim incur the penalty (jaza')? The answer hinges on the Hanafi principle of taghlib al-khatar—the predominance of prohibition. Let us unpack the ruling and its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the issue
Recognise that the act is hunting game (sayd) by a muhrim, and the specific issue is whether the animal's partial location inside the Haram triggers the penalty.
The Haram is the sacred precinct around Mecca where hunting is prohibited.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The ruling is clear: if any part of the game (e.g., a leg) is inside the Haram, the penalty becomes obligatory. It is not required that the entire animal be inside.
The text explicitly rejects the requirement that all legs be in the Haram.
3
Extract the legal cause ('illa)
The 'illa is taghlib al-khatar 'ala al-ibaha—the sanctity (khatar) of the Haram overrides the general permissibility (ibaha) of hunting in the Hal. Hence, even a small part inside invokes the full prohibition.
This is a qiyas-based reasoning: the Haram's sanctity is the effective cause.
4
Apply the 'illa to hypotheticals
Consider the case where the game is lying on the ground in the Hal with its head in the Haram. Since it is not wholly in the Hal, the penalty applies. Similarly, if the head is outside but legs are inside, penalty applies.
The principle is location-based, not majority-based.
5
Distinguish cases where no penalty applies
If the muhrim shoots from the Hal at game in the Hal without the arrow entering the Haram, there is no penalty. The same applies to sending a hunting dog or falcon from the Hal—the ruling is based on the game never being in the Haram.
The arrow or hunting animal entering the Haram alone does not transfer the sanctity to the game.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن ممر صرح به ورأسه في الحرم فلا شيء عليه في قتله ولا يشترط أن تكون جميع قوائمه في الحرم حتى لو كان بعض قوائمه في الحرم وبعضها في الحل وجب الجزاء بقتله لتغليب الخطر على الإباحة، ولهذا لو كان الصيد ملقى على الأرض في الحل ورأسه في الحرم وجب الجزاء بقتله لأنه ليس بقائم في الحل وبعضه في الحرم. وبما ذكرنا علم أنه لو رمى إلى صيد من الحل في الحل غير السهم في الحرم فإنه لا شيء عليه، وكذلك حكم الكلب والبازي إذا أرسلهما كما الأسبيجابي.
It has been explicitly stated: if a game has its head in the Haram, there is no penalty for killing it. It is not required that all its legs be in the Haram; so if some of its legs are in the Haram and some outside, the penalty becomes obligatory due to the predominance of prohibition over permissibility. For this reason, if the game is lying on the ground in the Hal with its head in the Haram, the penalty is obligatory because it is not standing entirely in the Hal while part of it is in the Haram. From what we have mentioned, it is understood that if one shoots at game from the Hal while in the Hal without the arrow [entering] the Haram, there is no penalty. Similarly, the ruling for a dog or falcon if sent [from outside] applies, as stated by al-Isbijabi.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage establishes the ruling for game partially inside the Haram. The key principle is that the location of the head alone does not subject the animal to Haram protection; rather, the presence of any part of the animal inside the Haram triggers the sanctity. The 'illa is the predominance of the Haram's sanctity (taghlib al-khatar 'ala al-ibaha). The passage also clarifies that shooting from the Hal without the arrow entering the Haram incurs no penalty, and the same applies to sending a hunting animal from outside. This ruling is specific to a muhrim (one in ihram) as the chapter heading indicates.

Key Terms
حرم (Haram): The sacred precinct of Mecca; legally, a protected area where hunting game is prohibited for a muhrim.
جزاء (jaza'): The penalty (fidyah) for killing game in the Haram, usually a sacrifice or equivalent value.
إباحة (ibaha): Permissibility; here, the permissibility of hunting in the Hal (outside Haram).
خطر (khatar): The sanctity and inviolability of the Haram which overrides the permissibility of hunting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a muhrim kills a deer that is lying in the Hal but has its head inside the Haram, what is the ruling?
A) No penalty because the body is in the Hal.
B) Penalty is obligatory because part of the deer is in the Haram.
C) The killing is permissible because the deer is not entirely in the Haram.
D) Only a warning is given, no penalty.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that any part of a game animal inside the Haram triggers the penalty for a muhrim, based on the predominance of sanctity over permissibility (taghlib al-khatar).

8 min
Shooting vs. Sending a Dog: Liability for Game Entering Haram
Intra-Hanafi dispute on attributing liability for game struck in Haram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between shooting and sending a dog for liability when game enters the Haram, and explain why istihsan overrides qiyas in shooting.

Core Idea
In hunting, liability depends on whether a free agent interrupts causation: arrow = yes, dog = no.

When a hunter shoots an arrow from outside the Haram and the arrow hits the game after it enters the Haram, must he pay a penalty? Abu Hanifa's general principle considers the time of the act, but here an exception is made for shooting based on istihsan. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this intra-Hanafi dispute and the crucial distinction between shooting and sending a dog.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Recognize that the passage contrasts shooting an arrow with sending a dog, both starting from the Hal, but with different outcomes regarding penalty.
In both, the game is killed in the Haram.
2
Contrast default qiyas with istihsan
Note that qiyas would exempt both from penalty because the initial act (shooting/sending) was permissible. But istihsan obligates penalty for shooting.
The exception is specific to shooting.
3
Pinpoint the illa (effective cause)
Analyze the difference: the arrow flies without a free agent's intervention, so the killing is attributed to the shooter. The dog acts independently, breaking attribution.
This is a khafiyyah (hidden) illa extracted by tanqih al-manat.
4
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical difference: in shooting, penalty is due; in sending a dog, no penalty, but the game is still unlawful to eat.
The ruling affects what the hunter must do (pay penalty) and what he may eat.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi position
Identify that the relied-upon position is the obligation of penalty for shooting, as per the istihsan reasoning transmitted by Muhammad al-Shaybani.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهل المعتبر حالة الرامي أو الإصابة؟ ففي فتاوى قاضيخان: لو رمى صيداً في الحل فنفر الصيد ووقع السهم في الحرم قال محمد عليه الجزاء في قول أبي حنيفة فيما أعلم اهـ. وذكر في المبسوط مثله في آخر المناسك، وذكر في موضع آخر أنه لا يلزمه الجزاء لأنه في الرمي غير مرتكب للنهي ولكن لا يحل تناول ذلك الصيد، وهذه المسألة المستثناة من أصل أبي حنيفة فإن عنده المعتبر حالة الرمي إلا في هذه المسألة خاصة فإنه يعتبر في حل التناول حالة الإصابة احتياطاً لأن الحل يحصل بالذكاة، وإنما يكون ذلك عند الإصابة وعلى هذا إرسال الكلب ا هـ. وقد اختلف كلامه لكن ذكر في البدائع أنه لا جزاء بأن وجوب مسكين عن الحموي : هذا عجيب منه فقد صرح به في متن النقاية حيث قال: وكذا ذبح الحلال صيد الحرم أو حلبه . قال الشراح: أي حلب الصيد فإنه يجب عليه قيمة اللبن ا هـ . قلت : وكذا في متن الملتقى قوله : (فإنه يعتبر في حل التناول حالة الإصابة تقييده بحل التناول يقتضي أن الاستثناء المذكور بالنسبة إليه لا بالنسبة إلى وجوب الجزاء وعدمه أن عبارة البدائع مصرح : مع الجزاء استحسان، وسيذكر المؤلف التوفيق بالحمل على الاستحسان فيكون الاستثناء مبنياً على الاستحسان وهو وجوب الجزاء لا حل التناول فتدبر وعبارة البدائع هكذا ولو أرسل كلباً في الحل على صيد في الحل فاتبعه الكلب فأخذه في الحرم فقتله لا شيء على المرسل، ولا يؤكل الصيد، أما الأول فلأن العبرة في وجوب الضمان لحالة الإرسال إذ هو السبب للضمان والإرسال وقع مباحاً فلا يتعلق به الضمان، وأما الثاني فلأن فعل الكلب ذبح للصيد وأنه حصل في الحرم فلا يحل أكله كما لو ذبحه آدمي إذ فعل الكلب لا يكون أعلى من فعل الآدمي. ولو رمى صيداً في الحل فنفر الصيد فوقع السهم به في الحرم فعليه الجزاء قال محمد في الأصل: وهو قول أبي حنيفة فيما أعلم، وكان القياس أن لا يجب عليه الجزاء كما في إرسال الكلب وخاصة على أصل أبي حنيفة فإنه يعتبر حالة الرمي في المسائل حتى قال فيمن رمي إلى مسلم فارتد المرمى إليه ثم أصابه السهم فقتله إنه يجب عليه الدية اعتباراً بحالة الرمي . إلا أنهم استحسنوا فأوجبوا الجزاء في الرمي دون الإرسال لأن الرمي المؤثر في الإصابة بمجرى العادة إن لم يتخلل بين الرمي والإصابة فعل فاعل مختار يقطع نسبة الأثر إليه شرعاً فبقيت الإصابة مضافة إليه شرعاً في الأحكام فصار كأنه ابتدأ الرمي بعد ما حصل الصيد في الحرم، وقد تخلل بين الإرسال والأخذ فعل فاعل على مختار وهو الكلب فمنع إضافة الأخذ إلى المرسل . ا هـ ملخصاً
And is the consideration the time of the shooter or the injury? In Fatawa Qadikhan: If he shot at game in the Hal, and the game fled and the arrow fell in the Haram, Muhammad said: He owes penalty according to Abu Hanifa, as far as I know. It is mentioned in al-Mabsut similarly at the end of al-Manasik, and it is mentioned elsewhere that no penalty is required because he did not commit a prohibition by shooting, but it is not lawful to consume that game. This issue is an exception from Abu Hanifa's general principle: with him, the time of shooting is considered, except in this particular issue, where he considers the time of injury for permission of consumption as a precaution, because permissibility of consumption is achieved by slaughter (dhabh), and that only occurs at the time of injury. And similarly for sending a dog. There is a difference in his words, but it is mentioned in al-Bada'i' that there is no penalty... al-Hamawi said: This is strange from him, for he stated explicitly in the text of al-Nuqayah: 'Similarly, if a non-muhrim slaughters game of the Haram or milks it.' The commentators said: i.e., milking the game, for which he owes the value of the milk. I say: Similarly in the text of al-Multaqa. His statement: 'He considers for permissibility of consumption the time of injury' – restricting it to permissibility of consumption indicates that the aforementioned exception pertains to that, not to the existence of penalty. The wording of al-Bada'i' states: 'The penalty is due by istihsan.' The author will later reconcile by attributing it to istihsan. So the exception is based on istihsan, which is the obligation of penalty, not permissibility of consumption. Contemplate. And the wording of al-Bada'i' is: If he sent a dog in the Hal at game in the Hal, and the dog followed it and caught it in the Haram and killed it, there is nothing on the sender, and the game is not eaten. As for the first, because the criterion for liability is the time of sending, for it is the cause of liability, and the sending occurred as permissible, so liability does not attach. As for the second, because the dog's action is slaughter of the game, and that occurred in the Haram, so it is not lawful to eat it, just as if a human had slaughtered it, for the dog's action is not superior to a human's action. If he shot at game in the Hal, and the game fled and the arrow struck it in the Haram, he owes penalty. Muhammad said in al-Asl: This is the saying of Abu Hanifa, as far as I know. The analogy (qiyas) would be that he should not owe penalty, as in sending a dog, especially according to Abu Hanifa's principle that he considers the time of shooting in issues, even saying regarding one who shot at a Muslim and the target apostatized then the arrow hit and killed him, that he must pay blood-money considering the time of shooting. However, they applied istihsan and obligated penalty in shooting but not in sending, because shooting effects the injury in the normal course unless between the shooting and the injury an act of a free agent intervenes that cuts off the attribution of the effect legally. So the injury remains attributed to him legally in rulings, and it becomes as if he began shooting after the game was in the Haram. In sending, between the sending and the taking, an act of a free agent – the dog – intervened, preventing attribution of the taking to the sender. End of summary.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage details an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding liability when a game is shot from outside the Haram but is hit after entering the Haram. Abu Hanifa's general principle considers the time of the action (shooting) as the basis for liability, but here an exception is made for shooting (ramy) based on istihsan, because the arrow's trajectory is continuous without a free agent's intervention, unlike sending a dog where the dog acts independently. Muhammad al-Shaybani reports Abu Hanifa's view that penalty is due, though the primary analogy would suggest no penalty. The distinction is that in shooting, the arrow's effect is attributed to the shooter even if the game moves into the Haram before impact, because no free agent breaks the causal chain. For the dog, the dog's own choice intervenes, so the owner is not liable for penalty, but the game remains unlawful to eat because the slaughter occurred in the Haram. The commentary also notes a disagreement in al-Bada'i' and clarifies that the exception only applies to the permissibility of consumption, not to the obligation of penalty, but the final position is that penalty is due by istihsan.

Key Terms
إستحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in cases of a stronger reason; here, it obligates penalty for shooting despite the general principle.
قيد (qayd): Restriction; here, the restriction of the exception to permissibility of consumption only, not to the obligation of penalty.
ضمان (daman): Liability; here, the legal responsibility to pay penalty or compensation for harming game.
ذكاة (dhabh): Ritual slaughter; the act that makes game lawful to eat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person shoots an arrow from the Hal at a deer, and the deer runs into the Haram where the arrow kills it, what is the ruling according to Abu Hanifa's relied-upon position?
A) No penalty because the shooting was permissible.
B) Penalty is obligatory because the arrow hit after the deer entered the Haram.
C) The deer may be eaten because the shooting was permissible.
D) Only a cautionary warning, no penalty.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, shooting an arrow from the Hal that kills game in the Haram obligates a penalty by istihsan, due to the continuous attribution of the act to the shooter.

Direct Causation: Scaring, Shouting, and Shooting Through Game in the Haram
Rulings on direct causation of game death in Haram.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that direct causation (mubashara) of death to game in the Haram incurs a penalty (damān), regardless of intent, distinguishing it from indirect causation.

Core Idea
Directly causing death to Haram game incurs penalty even if unintentional.

In this lesson, we examine three specific cases where a person's direct action—scaring, shouting, or shooting—causes the death of game within the Haram. The Hanafi school treats these as clear examples of mubashara, where liability is established without needing to prove transgression. Understanding these rulings clarifies the strict liability for direct harm in sacred precincts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act causing death
Each case involves a direct action: scaring (tafziʿ), shouting (ṣiyāḥ), or shooting (ramy) that hits and kills game.
Direct actions are those where the harm is an immediate result of the act.
2
Determine if causation is direct (mubashara)
The text states that scaring a game so it dies during flight, or shouting at it causing death, are direct causes. Similarly, an arrow that passes through one game and kills another is direct.
In all these, no intermediary beyond the initial act.
3
Apply the ruling: liability (damān) is incurred
The text clearly says 'yḍman' (he is liable). The penalty is wajib (obligatory) as a form of compensation for violating the sanctity of the Haram.
Intent and error are equal in this chapter, as explicitly mentioned for the shooting-through case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صبي وهنا فروع لم أرها صريحاً في كلام أئمتنا وإن أمكن استخراجها منه منها : لو نفر صيداً فهلك في حال هربه ونفاره وينبغي أن يكون ضامناً ولا يخرج عن العهدة حتى يسكن. ومنها لو صاح على صيد فمات من صياحه يضمن وينبغي أن يقاس على ما إذا صاح على فمات. ومنها ما لو رمى إلى صيد فنفذ فيه السهم فأصاب صيداً آخر فقتلهما فينبغي أن يلزمه جزاءان لأن العمد والخطأ في هذا الباب سواء وهم قد صرحوا به في صيد الحرم ومنها إذا حفر بئراً فهلك فيها صيد الحرم وينبغي أنه إذا كان في ملكه أو موات لا ضمان وإلا ضمن بناء على أن التسبب يشترط فيه التعدي للماء لا يضمن وإن كان للاصطياد يضمن . ومنها لو جرح الحلال صيداً في الحل ثم دخل الصيد الحرم فجرحه فمات منها وينبغي أن يلزمه قيمته مجروحاً كما تقدم في صيد الحرم. ومنها لو أمسك صيداً في الحل وله فرخ في الحرم فماتا ضمن الفرخ لا الأم ا اه. ثم بعد ذلك: ولو أرسل بازياً في الحل فدخل من غير قصد مرسله الحرم فقتل صيداً لا شيء عليه، ولو أرسل كلباً على ذئب في الحرم أو نصب له شبكة فأصاب الكلب صيداً أو وقع في الشبكة صيد فلا جزاء عليه أي لأن قصده قتل الذئب الذي هو حلال له فلم يكن متعدياً ا هـ . شارح ولو نصبها للصيد فعليه الجزاء ولو نصب خيمة فتعلق به صيد أو حفر للماء فوقع فيه صيد لا ضمان عليه، ولو أمسك حلال صيداً في الحل وله فرخ في الحرم فماتا ضمن الفرخ لا الأم ا اه. قوله : (ومنها إذا حفر بئراً فهلك فيها صيد الحرم كذا في بعض النسخ. وفي بعضها زيادة وهي: وينبغي أنه إن كان في ملكه أو موات لا ضمان وإلا ضمن قوله : (ثم دخل الصيد الحرم فجرحه فمات منها كذا في هذه النسخة موافقاً لما في النهر، وفي عدة نسخ غيرها بدون فجرحه والظاهر ما هنا تأمل قوله : (ومنها لو أمسك صيداً في الحل الخ) قال في النهر : هذه المسألة تعرف مما مر فيما لو غلقا الباب على صيد قمات عطشاً ا هـ . قلت : وكذا من مسألة ما لو نفر صيداً عن
[Before the branches:] ... Here are branches that I have not seen explicitly in the words of our imams, although they can be extracted from them. Among them: If he scares a game so that it dies in the state of its fleeing and scaring, then he should be liable (daman) and not be absolved until it settles. Among them: If he shouts at a game and it dies from his shouting, he is liable, and it should be analogized to the case of shouting at a human and he dies. Among them: If he shoots at a game, and the arrow penetrates it and hits another game, killing both, then he should owe two penalties, because intent and error are equal in this chapter, and they have stated this regarding game of the Haram. Among them: If he digs a well and a game of the Haram perishes in it, then if it was in his property or dead land, no liability; otherwise he is liable, based on the principle that indirect causation requires transgression. For water, no liability; but if for hunting, he is liable. Among them: If a non-muhrim wounds a game in the Hal, then the game enters the Haram and he wounds it again and it dies from that, then he should owe its value as wounded, as earlier in game of the Haram. Among them: If he holds a game in the Hal while it has a chick in the Haram, and both die, he is liable for the chick, not the mother. Then after that: If he sent a falcon from the Hal and it entered the Haram without the intention of the sender and killed game, there is nothing on him. If he sent a dog at a wolf in the Haram or set a net for it, and the dog hits game or a game falls into the net, then no penalty on him, because his intention was to kill the wolf, which is lawful for him, so he was not transgressing. The commentator said: If he set it for hunting, then penalty is due. If he pitched a tent and a game got caught on it, or dug for water and a game fell in, no liability. If a non-muhrim held a game in the Hal while it had a chick in the Haram and both died, he is liable for the chick, not the mother. His statement: 'Among them, if he dug a well...' is in some copies, and in some there is an addition: 'And it should be that if it was in his property or dead land, no liability, otherwise liable.' His statement: 'Then the game entered the Haram and he wounded it and it died from that' is in this copy agreeing with al-Nahr, and in several other copies without 'and he wounded it' and the apparent is what is here. Contemplate. His statement: 'Among them, if he held a game in the Hal...' al-Nahr said: This issue is known from what passed regarding if they closed the door on a game and it died of thirst. I say: Similarly from the issue of scaring a game...
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage collects various subsidiary rulings (furu') about causing harm to game in the Haram, whether directly or indirectly. Key principles: (1) Scaring or shouting causing death incurs liability because direct causation (mubashara) is present. (2) Shooting through one game into another requires penalties for both, as intent and error are equal in Haram violations. (3) Digging a well: liability depends on location (owned or dead land vs. public) and purpose (water vs. hunting). (4) Wounding game outside then inside: value assessed as wounded. (5) If a game has offspring in the Haram, only the offspring's loss is compensable if the mother is caught outside. (6) Sending a falcon or dog without intention to hunt in Haram: no penalty if the animal acts independently. (7) Setting a net or tent: liability if intended for hunting; otherwise no liability for accidental catches. (8) The final comments discuss textual variants. These rulings are based on qiyas and tasabbub principles unique to Hanafi fiqh regarding the sanctity of the Haram.

Key Terms
مباشرة (mubashara): Direct causation; here, causing death directly through one's action (e.g., shouting, scaring).
جزاء (jazā'): Penalty or compensation for violating the sanctity of the Haram by killing game.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person shoots an arrow at a rabbit in the Haram, the arrow misses and kills a bird, what is the ruling?
A) No penalty because the target was rabbit.
B) One penalty for the bird because intent was for rabbit.
C) One penalty because arrow directly killed bird.
D) Two penalties because intent and error are equal.

Key Takeaway
Direct causation (mubashara) of death to game in the Haram incurs a penalty regardless of intent.

Indirect Causation: Digging Wells and Setting Nets in the Haram
Rulings on indirect causation (tasabbub) for game in Haram.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that indirect causation (tasabbub) of harm to game in the Haram only incurs liability if accompanied by transgression (ta'addi), distinguishing between permissible and impermissible purposes.

Core Idea
Indirect harm to Haram game is penalized only when the act involves transgression.

This lesson examines two types of indirect actions: digging a well and setting a net (or tent). The Hanafi position is that liability depends on location and purpose. If the act is done on one's own property or dead land (mawat) for a legitimate purpose like water, no penalty arises. But if done for hunting or on another's land with transgression, liability attaches. This distinguishes indirect from direct causation.

Guided Path
1
Classify the act as indirect (tasabbub)
Digging a well or setting a net does not directly cause death; the animal falls in later. This is indirect causation.
Indirect means the harm occurs through an intermediary (the well/net).
2
Check for transgression (ta'addi)
Transgression exists if the act is done where it is not allowed (e.g., someone else's land) or for a prohibited purpose (e.g., hunting in the Haram).
The text mentions location (property vs. other) and purpose (water vs. hunting).
3
Apply the ruling accordingly
If no transgression, no liability. If transgression exists, liability (damān) is imposed.
The text gives explicit examples: well on own land for water → no penalty; well on public land for hunting → penalty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صبي وهنا فروع لم أرها صريحاً في كلام أئمتنا وإن أمكن استخراجها منه منها : لو نفر صيداً فهلك في حال هربه ونفاره وينبغي أن يكون ضامناً ولا يخرج عن العهدة حتى يسكن. ومنها لو صاح على صيد فمات من صياحه يضمن وينبغي أن يقاس على ما إذا صاح على فمات. ومنها ما لو رمى إلى صيد فنفذ فيه السهم فأصاب صيداً آخر فقتلهما فينبغي أن يلزمه جزاءان لأن العمد والخطأ في هذا الباب سواء وهم قد صرحوا به في صيد الحرم ومنها إذا حفر بئراً فهلك فيها صيد الحرم وينبغي أنه إذا كان في ملكه أو موات لا ضمان وإلا ضمن بناء على أن التسبب يشترط فيه التعدي للماء لا يضمن وإن كان للاصطياد يضمن . ومنها لو جرح الحلال صيداً في الحل ثم دخل الصيد الحرم فجرحه فمات منها وينبغي أن يلزمه قيمته مجروحاً كما تقدم في صيد الحرم. ومنها لو أمسك صيداً في الحل وله فرخ في الحرم فماتا ضمن الفرخ لا الأم ا اه. ثم بعد ذلك: ولو أرسل بازياً في الحل فدخل من غير قصد مرسله الحرم فقتل صيداً لا شيء عليه، ولو أرسل كلباً على ذئب في الحرم أو نصب له شبكة فأصاب الكلب صيداً أو وقع في الشبكة صيد فلا جزاء عليه أي لأن قصده قتل الذئب الذي هو حلال له فلم يكن متعدياً ا هـ . شارح ولو نصبها للصيد فعليه الجزاء ولو نصب خيمة فتعلق به صيد أو حفر للماء فوقع فيه صيد لا ضمان عليه، ولو أمسك حلال صيداً في الحل وله فرخ في الحرم فماتا ضمن الفرخ لا الأم ا اه. قوله : (ومنها إذا حفر بئراً فهلك فيها صيد الحرم كذا في بعض النسخ. وفي بعضها زيادة وهي: وينبغي أنه إن كان في ملكه أو موات لا ضمان وإلا ضمن قوله : (ثم دخل الصيد الحرم فجرحه فمات منها كذا في هذه النسخة موافقاً لما في النهر، وفي عدة نسخ غيرها بدون فجرحه والظاهر ما هنا تأمل قوله : (ومنها لو أمسك صيداً في الحل الخ) قال في النهر : هذه المسألة تعرف مما مر فيما لو غلقا الباب على صيد قمات عطشاً ا هـ . قلت : وكذا من مسألة ما لو نفر صيداً عن
[Before the branches:] ... Here are branches that I have not seen explicitly in the words of our imams, although they can be extracted from them. Among them: If he scares a game so that it dies in the state of its fleeing and scaring, then he should be liable (daman) and not be absolved until it settles. Among them: If he shouts at a game and it dies from his shouting, he is liable, and it should be analogized to the case of shouting at a human and he dies. Among them: If he shoots at a game, and the arrow penetrates it and hits another game, killing both, then he should owe two penalties, because intent and error are equal in this chapter, and they have stated this regarding game of the Haram. Among them: If he digs a well and a game of the Haram perishes in it, then if it was in his property or dead land, no liability; otherwise he is liable, based on the principle that indirect causation requires transgression. For water, no liability; but if for hunting, he is liable. Among them: If a non-muhrim wounds a game in the Hal, then the game enters the Haram and he wounds it again and it dies from that, then he should owe its value as wounded, as earlier in game of the Haram. Among them: If he holds a game in the Hal while it has a chick in the Haram, and both die, he is liable for the chick, not the mother. Then after that: If he sent a falcon from the Hal and it entered the Haram without the intention of the sender and killed game, there is nothing on him. If he sent a dog at a wolf in the Haram or set a net for it, and the dog hits game or a game falls into the net, then no penalty on him, because his intention was to kill the wolf, which is lawful for him, so he was not transgressing. The commentator said: If he set it for hunting, then penalty is due. If he pitched a tent and a game got caught on it, or dug for water and a game fell in, no liability. If a non-muhrim held a game in the Hal while it had a chick in the Haram and both died, he is liable for the chick, not the mother. His statement: 'Among them, if he dug a well...' is in some copies, and in some there is an addition: 'And it should be that if it was in his property or dead land, no liability, otherwise liable.' His statement: 'Then the game entered the Haram and he wounded it and it died from that' is in this copy agreeing with al-Nahr, and in several other copies without 'and he wounded it' and the apparent is what is here. Contemplate. His statement: 'Among them, if he held a game in the Hal...' al-Nahr said: This issue is known from what passed regarding if they closed the door on a game and it died of thirst. I say: Similarly from the issue of scaring a game...
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage collects various subsidiary rulings (furu') about causing harm to game in the Haram, whether directly or indirectly. Key principles: (1) Scaring or shouting causing death incurs liability because direct causation (mubashara) is present. (2) Shooting through one game into another requires penalties for both, as intent and error are equal in Haram violations. (3) Digging a well: liability depends on location (owned or dead land vs. public) and purpose (water vs. hunting). (4) Wounding game outside then inside: value assessed as wounded. (5) If a game has offspring in the Haram, only the offspring's loss is compensable if the mother is caught outside. (6) Sending a falcon or dog without intention to hunt in Haram: no penalty if the animal acts independently. (7) Setting a net or tent: liability if intended for hunting; otherwise no liability for accidental catches. (8) The final comments discuss textual variants. These rulings are based on qiyas and tasabbub principles unique to Hanafi fiqh regarding the sanctity of the Haram.

Key Terms
تسبب (tasabbub): Indirect causation; here, actions like digging a well that indirectly cause harm.
تعدي (ta'addi): Transgression; acting beyond one's right, e.g., digging a well in someone else's property.
موات (mawat): Dead land; unowned barren land where no one has ownership rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man digs a well in the Haram on public land to water his livestock. A gazelle falls in and dies. What is the ruling?
A) No liability because the purpose is lawful.
B) Liability because it is on public land.
C) Liability because any well in Haram is prohibited.
D) No liability because it is for water.

Key Takeaway
Indirect causation (tasabbub) only incurs liability for Haram game when accompanied by transgression (ta'addi).

Actions of Hunting Animals and Special Cases (Mother-Chick, Wounded Outside)
Rulings on independent animal actions and special cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that if a hunting animal (falcon, dog) acts independently without the owner's intent to hunt in the Haram, no penalty is due, and also distinguish the special rules for mother-chick and pre-wounded game.

Core Idea
Liability for game in Haram depends on human intention and causation, not on the animal's independent action.

This lesson covers two additional categories: first, when a hunting animal sent from the Hal enters the Haram and kills game without the owner's intention, no penalty arises because the owner did not transgress. Second, special cases: if a game has a chick in the Haram and the mother is caught outside, only the chick's death incurs liability; and if a game is wounded outside then finished inside, the value is based on its wounded state. These rulings refine the principles of intent and directness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario: hunting animal sent without Haram intent
The owner sends a falcon or dog from the Hal (outside the Haram) to hunt a lawful target (wolf) but the animal enters the Haram and kills protected game.
Key: the owner did not intend to hunt in the Haram.
2
Apply the rule: no penalty if no transgression
The text explicitly says 'no penalty' because the owner's intention was lawful (killing a wolf) and he did not commit ta'addi.
Contrast: if he set the animal or net intentionally for hunting in Haram, penalty due.
3
Special case 1: Mother caught outside with chick in Haram
If a non-muhrim catches a game outside (Hal) and it has a chick in the Haram, and both die, liability is only for the chick, not the mother, because the mother's death was outside the Haram's domain.
The chick's sanctity is tied to the Haram.
4
Special case 2: Game wounded outside, finished inside
If a non-muhrim wounds a game outside, then it enters the Haram and he wounds it again (or it dies from the first wound), he owes its value as a wounded animal (reduced value), as per the previous chapter on game of the Haram.
The ruling accounts for the diminished value before full death.

Source Text

صبي وهنا فروع لم أرها صريحاً في كلام أئمتنا وإن أمكن استخراجها منه منها : لو نفر صيداً فهلك في حال هربه ونفاره وينبغي أن يكون ضامناً ولا يخرج عن العهدة حتى يسكن. ومنها لو صاح على صيد فمات من صياحه يضمن وينبغي أن يقاس على ما إذا صاح على فمات. ومنها ما لو رمى إلى صيد فنفذ فيه السهم فأصاب صيداً آخر فقتلهما فينبغي أن يلزمه جزاءان لأن العمد والخطأ في هذا الباب سواء وهم قد صرحوا به في صيد الحرم ومنها إذا حفر بئراً فهلك فيها صيد الحرم وينبغي أنه إذا كان في ملكه أو موات لا ضمان وإلا ضمن بناء على أن التسبب يشترط فيه التعدي للماء لا يضمن وإن كان للاصطياد يضمن . ومنها لو جرح الحلال صيداً في الحل ثم دخل الصيد الحرم فجرحه فمات منها وينبغي أن يلزمه قيمته مجروحاً كما تقدم في صيد الحرم. ومنها لو أمسك صيداً في الحل وله فرخ في الحرم فماتا ضمن الفرخ لا الأم ا اه. ثم بعد ذلك: ولو أرسل بازياً في الحل فدخل من غير قصد مرسله الحرم فقتل صيداً لا شيء عليه، ولو أرسل كلباً على ذئب في الحرم أو نصب له شبكة فأصاب الكلب صيداً أو وقع في الشبكة صيد فلا جزاء عليه أي لأن قصده قتل الذئب الذي هو حلال له فلم يكن متعدياً ا هـ . شارح ولو نصبها للصيد فعليه الجزاء ولو نصب خيمة فتعلق به صيد أو حفر للماء فوقع فيه صيد لا ضمان عليه، ولو أمسك حلال صيداً في الحل وله فرخ في الحرم فماتا ضمن الفرخ لا الأم ا اه. قوله : (ومنها إذا حفر بئراً فهلك فيها صيد الحرم كذا في بعض النسخ. وفي بعضها زيادة وهي: وينبغي أنه إن كان في ملكه أو موات لا ضمان وإلا ضمن قوله : (ثم دخل الصيد الحرم فجرحه فمات منها كذا في هذه النسخة موافقاً لما في النهر، وفي عدة نسخ غيرها بدون فجرحه والظاهر ما هنا تأمل قوله : (ومنها لو أمسك صيداً في الحل الخ) قال في النهر : هذه المسألة تعرف مما مر فيما لو غلقا الباب على صيد قمات عطشاً ا هـ . قلت : وكذا من مسألة ما لو نفر صيداً عن

Game on Overhanging Branches: Place vs. Root
Resolves a dispute between qiyas and istihsan for game on overhanging branches.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing game on branches overhanging the Haram and explain why istihsan overrides qiyas in this case.

Core Idea
For game, location matters; for a tree, its root matters—istihsan refines the qiyas analogy.

This passage tackles a subtle scenario: a tree rooted in the Halal (outside the Haram) has branches extending into the Haram. If game is on those branches, does killing it incur the Haram's penalty? A qiyas approach would look at the tree's root, but istihsan looks at the game's physical location. The author resolves the dispute by distinguishing the game from the tree itself, and extends the ruling to birds in the airspace.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested scenario
The scenario: tree root in Halal, branches overhang into Haram, game is on those branches. The question: does killing the game require the Haram penalty (jaza')?
The root being Halal might suggest no penalty by analogy with cutting the tree.
2
Contrast the two analogies: qiyas and istihsan
Qiyas analogizes the game to the tree: since the root is Halal, the entire tree is Halal, so the game should be Halal. Istihsan looks at the game's location: it is physically in the Haram, even if indirectly, so the penalty applies.
The author adopts istihsan, breaking the qiyas due to a key difference.
3
Locate the distinguishing factor
The author explains: branches are dependent on the tree (tabi'), so the tree's root governs the branch's ruling. But game is not dependent on the tree; its own location determines its ruling.
This is the core illa for the distinction.
4
Extend to airspace
The author further clarifies that 'being in the Haram' does not require being on the ground. A bird flying in the airspace is considered in the Haram, citing Quran 3:97. Airspace is like the land.
This closes a potential loophole.
5
State the final mufta bihi ruling
The penalty (jaza') is wajib for killing game on overhanging branches or in the Haram's airspace. This is the istihsan position.
The author reconciles by keeping qiyas for cutting the tree but applying istihsan for game.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبما ذكرنا علم أن الصيد لو كان على أغصان شجرة متدلية في الحرم وأصل الشجرة في الحل فإن قتله عليه الجزاء لأن المعتبر في الصيد مكانه لا أصله، وفي حرمة قطع الشجرة العبرة للأصل لا للأغصان لأن الأغصان تبع للشجرة وليس الصيد تبعاً لها وهكذا في المحيط وغيره وليس المراد من كون الصيد في الحرم أن يكون في أرضه لأنه لا يشترط الكون في الأرض لأنه لو كان طائراً في الحرم وليس في الأرض فإنه من صيد الحرم لأنه دخله وقد قال تعالى ومن دخله كان آمناً [آل عمران : ٩٧] وهواء الحرم كالحرم.
And by what we have mentioned, it is known that if the game was on the branches of a tree overhanging in the Haram while the root of the tree is in the Halal, then if he kills it, the penalty is due upon him, because the consideration in game is its place, not its origin. And regarding the prohibition of cutting the tree, the consideration is for the origin, not for the branches, because the branches are dependent on the tree, and the game is not dependent on it. This is how it is in al-Muhit and elsewhere. And the intention of the game being in the Haram is not that it be on its ground, because being on the ground is not a condition; for if it were a bird in the Haram and not on the ground, then it is from the game of the Haram, because it entered it, and Allah Most High said: 'And whoever enters it is safe' [Al-Imran: 97]. And the airspace of the Haram is like the Haram.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute resolved by the author. It begins by stating the reconciled ruling: if game is on branches overhanging the Haram but the tree's root is in the Halal, the penalty for killing it is wajib. The reasoning is that for game, the relevant consideration is its location at the time of killing (the place where it is found), not the origin of the tree. This is contrasted with cutting the tree itself, where the origin (the root) matters because branches are dependent on the root. The author supports this with references to al-Muhit and other sources. He further explains that the game being in the Haram does not require it to be on the ground; a bird in the airspace of the Haram is considered within the Haram, citing the Quranic verse (3:97) that whoever enters it is safe. Thus, the airspace shares the same sanctity as the land. This ruling applies the istihsan position that penalty is due, overriding the qiyas that would have exempted it.

Key Terms
صيد (sayd): Game animal that is hunted; in context, any wild animal that is permissible to hunt outside the Haram.
جزاء (jaza'): The penalty due for killing game in the Haram or while in ihram; typically a sacrificial animal or its value.
هواء الحرم (hawa' al-haram): The airspace above the Haram; considered part of the Haram for rulings on sanctity and game.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Both follow the root of the tree.
B) Game follows its place; the tree follows its root.
C) Neither follows the root; both follow the branches.
D) The tree is not considered part of the Haram.

Key Takeaway
For game in the Haram, its physical location (including airspace) determines the penalty, not the origin of any object it rests on.

Branches of Rulings on Game in the Haram
Deduced rulings on liability for game in Haram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for liability in causing death of game in the Haram and apply them to various scenarios of direct and indirect causation.

Core Idea
In the Haram, both intentional and accidental killing of game incur liability; indirect causation requires transgression.

This passage presents several deduced rulings (furu') regarding game in the Haram, derived from general Hanafi principles. The author (Ibn Nujaym) explores scenarios such as frightening or shouting at game, shooting through game, digging wells, and boundary cases between Halal and Haram. Understanding these branches helps us apply the key concepts of causation (tasabbub) and transgression (ta'addī) in determining liability.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general principle
Recognize that in the Haram, both intentional and accidental killing of game are treated similarly regarding liability (damān). This is a strict rule to protect the sanctity of the Haram.
2
Analyze direct causation branches
Examine the first two rulings: frightening game or shouting at it causing death. Identify that both are direct acts (mubāshara) leading to liability without needing transgression beyond the act itself.
3
Examine the shooting-through branch
When an arrow passes through one game and kills another, two penalties apply because the act caused two deaths, each treated as an independent killing in the Haram.
4
Analyze indirect causation via well-digging
Digging a well that leads to game death: liability depends on transgression (ta'addī). If dug on own property or dead land (mawāt), no liability; otherwise, liable. Additionally, a water well without hunting intent does not incur liability, but a hunting well does.
5
Evaluate boundary cases
Review the rulings involving boundaries: a Halal person injures game outside Haram, then it enters Haram and is injured again – liable for value as injured. Catching a mother in Halal with chick in Haram: liable for chick only (since it was in Haram).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهنا فروع لم أرها صريحاً في كلام أئمتنا وإن أمكن استخراجها منه منها : لو نفر صيداً فهلك في حال هربه ونفاره وينبغي أن يكون ضامناً ولا يخرج عن العهدة حتى يسكن. ومنها لو صاح على صيد فمات من صياحه يضمن وينبغي أن يقاس على ما إذا صاح على فمات. ومنها ما لو رمى إلى صيد فنفذ فيه السهم فأصاب صيداً آخر فقتلهما فينبغي أن يلزمه جزاءان لأن العمد والخطأ في هذا الباب سواء وهم قد صرحوا به في صيد الحرم ومنها إذا حفر بئراً فهلك فيها صيد الحرم وينبغي أنه إذا كان في ملكه أو موات لا ضمان وإلا ضمن بناء على أن التسبب يشترط فيه التعدي للماء لا يضمن وإن كان للاصطياد يضمن . ومنها لو جرح الحلال صيداً في الحل ثم دخل الصيد الحرم فجرحه فمات منها وينبغي أن يلزمه قيمته مجروحاً كما تقدم في صيد الحرم. ومنها لو أمسك صيداً في الحل وله فرخ في الحرم فماتا ضمن الفرخ لا الأم. ومنها لو أمسك صيداً في الحل وله فرخ في الحرم فماتا ضمن الفرخ لا الأم.
And here are branches that I have not seen explicitly in the words of our Imams, although it is possible to extract them from them. Among them: If he frightens game and it dies during its flight and disturbance, he should be liable and does not exit from responsibility until it settles. Among them: If he shouts at game and it dies from his shout, he is liable, and it should be analogized to the case when he shouts at [an animal] and it dies. Among them: If he shoots an arrow at game and the arrow penetrates it and hits another game and kills both, then he should be liable for two penalties, because intentional and error in this chapter are the same; and they have explicitly stated this regarding the game of the Haram. Among them: If he digs a well and game of the Haram perishes in it: it should be that if it is in his property or dead land, there is no liability; otherwise, he is liable, based on the fact that for causation, transgression is required; for water, he is not liable, but if it is for hunting, he is liable. Among them: If a Halal person injures game in the Halal, then the game enters the Haram and he injures it and it dies from that, he should be liable for its value as injured, as previously mentioned regarding the game of the Haram. Among them: If he catches game in the Halal and the game has a chick in the Haram, and both die, he is liable for the chick, not the mother.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage enumerates a series of derived rulings (furu') concerning game in the Haram, deduced by the author from general principles even if not explicitly stated in the classical texts. The first ruling: if one frightens game causing it to die during flight, he is liable (damān) and remains liable until the game settles. The second: shouting at game that causes its death incurs liability, analogized to shouting at another animal. Third: if an arrow passes through one game and kills a second, two penalties are owed because in the Haram, both intentional and accidental killing have the same ruling. Fourth: digging a well in which game perishes: no liability if the well is on one's own property or dead land (mawāt), but if it is on someone else's property or public land, liability applies, provided the digging was without transgression; for a water well, there is no liability unless dug for hunting. Fifth: if a Halal person injures game outside the Haram, the game then enters the Haram, and a second injury causes death, he owes the value of the game in its injured state. Sixth: if one catches a mother game in the Halal that has a chick in the Haram, and both die, he is liable for the chick (being in the Haram) but not for the mother. These rulings illustrate that causation (tasabbub) without transgression may not incur liability, but transgression in the Haram triggers strong consequences.

Key Terms
ضامن (dāmin): Liable to pay compensation or penalty; in this context, for causing the death of game in the Haram.
تسبب (tasabbub): Causation through indirect means, e.g., digging a well that leads to death. Liability arises only if the act is transgressive (mu'tadī).
تعدي (ta'addī): Transgression; acting without right or beyond bounds, which is a condition for liability in tasabbub.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a Halal person digs a well on his own property in the Halal, and a Haram game animal falls in and dies, what is the ruling according to this passage?
A) He is liable because it is game of the Haram.
B) He is not liable because there is no transgression.
C) He is liable only if he dug for hunting.
D) He is liable regardless of intention.

Key Takeaway
Liability for killing game in the Haram arises from any causal act (direct or indirect) that leads to death, provided that indirect causation involves transgression (ta'addī); intention and error are treated equally.

Haram Boundaries: Transmission, Disagreement, and the Method of Knowing
Intra-Hanafi dispute over Haram boundary distances

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi reliance on transmission (naql) for determining the Haram boundaries and evaluate the intra-Hanafi criticism regarding inconsistent measurements.

Core Idea
Haram boundaries are known by transmission, not reason; disagreement exists even among Hanafi authorities.

This passage delves into the precise boundaries of the sacred precinct (Haram) of Makkah, a matter of great practical importance for applying Haram-specific rulings. The Hanafi school holds that these boundaries are known exclusively through transmission (naql) from the prophets, not through analogy. However, as the passage shows, there is internal disagreement over the exact distances, highlighting the critical methodology later Hanafi scholars employed even toward earlier authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimed boundary measurements
Read the passage from al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah: the distances from four sides are 6, 12, 18, and 24 miles respectively. Note these are attributed to al-Faqih Abu Ja'far.
2
Recognize the epistemological basis
The text explicitly states that these measurements are known by naql (transmission), not qiyas (analogy). Understand that in Hanafi usul, matters like boundary markers are based on transmitted texts, not independent reasoning.
This is a key distinction: Haram boundaries are a matter of riwayah, not dirayah.
3
Analyze al-Sadr al-Shahid's criticism
Al-Sadr al-Shahid points out a practical inconsistency: the miqat of Umrah at al-Tan'im is only about 3 miles from Makkah, contradicting the 12 miles claimed for the second side. Understand that this is a critique based on observable reality.
This shows the later Hanafi method of verifying transmitted information through known landmarks.
4
Compare with al-Nawawi's detailed account
Al-Nawawi provides a more precise set of distances from each direction (e.g., 3 miles from Madinah, 7 from Yemen, etc.) and mentions physical markers. Consider how his account offers a more granular and seemingly more accurate picture.
Al-Nawawi was a Shafi'i scholar, but his data is cited here, showing cross-madhhab reference.
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The dispute matters because the Haram has specific rulings (e.g., hunting prohibitions, tree cutting, and penalties). An incorrect boundary can lead to wrong application of these rules.
This highlights why later Hanafi scholars scrutinized earlier transmissions.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah and others: the measure of the Haram from the east is six miles, from the second side twelve miles, from the third side eighteen miles, and from the fourth side twenty-four miles. Thus said al-Faqih Abu Ja'far. And this is something not known by analogy, but only known by transmission. Al-Sadr al-Shahid said: there is a problem in what he said, because from the second side is the miqat of Umrah which is al-Tan'im, and that is close to three miles. And Imam al-Nawawi mentioned in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab that its limit from the direction of Madinah is three miles from Makkah at al-Tan'im, from the direction of Yemen seven miles from Makkah, from the direction of Ta'if on the road to Arafat from Batn Namira seven miles, from the direction of Iraq on the mountain pass of al-Maqta' seven miles, from the direction of Ji'ranah in the valley of Abu Abdullah Khalid ibn Ali nine miles, and from the direction of Jeddah ten miles from Makkah. And that there are markers erected on all its sides, set up by Ibrahim al-Khalil (AS) and Jibril showed him their locations, then the Prophet (SAW) ordered their renewal, then Uthman, then Mu'awiyah (RA), and they are still visible until now. And Qadi Abu al-Fadl al-Nuwairi collected them and said: 'For the Haram the limit from the land of Tayyibah is three miles if you desire its accuracy...' Then I saw the issue explicitly stated in the text of al-Lubab: 'If both die, he is liable for the chick, not the mother.' His statement: 'If we say that his holding back from its chick is a sin' – in some copies it is 'from the Halal' instead of 'from its chick', and its meaning is not clear to me. And he tied it with what was previously mentioned that the cause is like direct action on condition that it be transgression. His statement: 'And among them: if he stands on a branch in the Halal...' He said in al-Nahr: in al-Siraj: 'If the shooter is in the Haram and the game in the Halal, or vice versa, it is from the game of the Haram. And if he shoots at game in the Halal and it flees and is hit in the Haram, he owes the penalty. And if it is hit in the Halal and dies in the Haram, it is permissible to eat it according to qiyas, but disliked according to istihsan.' His statement: 'And among them: if a Halal person sees game while sitting in the Haram...' He said in al-Nahr: 'One should not hesitate in its permissibility, as there is no prohibition there.' His statement: 'And from Yaman seven... to the end.'
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a rich composite. It begins by citing al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah on the boundaries of the Haram according to al-Faqih Abu Ja'far: 6 miles east, 12 miles second side, 18 miles third, 24 miles fourth. Al-Sadr al-Shahid criticizes this, noting that the miqat of Umrah (al-Tan'im) is only about 3 miles from Makkah. Then Imam al-Nawawi's more detailed account from Sharh al-Muhadhdhab gives specific measurements from each direction (Madinah: 3 miles, Yemen: 7, Ta'if: 7, Iraq: 7, Ji'ranah: 9, Jeddah: 10). These are known by transmission from Ibrahim (AS) and renewed later. The author also notes a poem by Qadi al-Nuwairi. Then he returns to the previous furu' branches, referencing al-Lubab to confirm the ruling about the chick. He discusses a variant reading in the text and comments on the principle that causation (sabab) is like direct action if it involves transgression (udwan). He further cites al-Nahr from al-Siraj on the shooter's position relative to the Haram, and notes the istihsan dislike for eating game killed in Halal but dying in Haram. The passage ends abruptly with a heading 'And from Yaman seven...' indicating continuation. This section demonstrates the textual transmission basis for Haram boundaries and the critical methodology of later Hanafi scholars.

Key Terms
حد الحرم (hadd al-haram): The territorial boundary of the sacred precinct (Haram) of Makkah, with specific distances from various directions.
ميقات العمرة (miqat al-umrah): The station for assuming ihram for Umrah; al-Tan'im is mentioned as being close to the Haram boundary.
نقل (naql): Transmission of knowledge through reliable chains, as opposed to independent reasoning (qiyas).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the epistemological basis for knowing the boundaries of the Haram?
A) Qiyas (analogy) based on the distance of Mina
B) Naql (transmission) from the prophets and later renewals
C) Ijma (consensus) of the Companions
D) Istihsan (juristic preference) of the later scholars

Key Takeaway
The Haram boundaries are a matter of divine transmission, and the existence of intra-Hanafi disagreement shows the importance of critically examining relied-upon reports while preserving the principle that these limits are not subject to analogical reasoning.

Liability for Killing Game in the Haram
Expansion on liability for game in various scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on liability for game in the Haram by analyzing causation and the condition of transgression.

Core Idea
Liability for game attaches when the cause is a transgression (ma'siya).

This lesson examines several scenarios from the text where a person causes the death of game in the Haram. The key principle is that a cause (sabab) is treated like direct action only when the cause itself is a sin. We will work through the examples to see how this principle operates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and location
Determine what the person did (killed, prevented, sent a bird) and whether the act occurred inside or outside the Haram.
Location matters: game in the Haram is protected.
2
Trace causation
Is the death directly caused by the person's action, or is it a consequence (e.g., chick dying because mother prevented)?
Cause (sabab) is distinct from direct action (mubashara).
3
Check the transgression condition
For the cause to be like direct action, the cause itself must be a transgression (ma'siya). If not, liability may not attach.
Example: preventing a bird from reaching its chick may or may not be a sin depending on intent.
4
Apply the ruling
Based on the above, state whether liability (daman) applies: fidyah or compensation.
Liability is typically a penalty (jaza') in kind or value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ورمى إلى صيد في الحل أو كان الغصن في الحرم والشجرة والصيد في الحل، وينبغي أن يكون الواقف على الغصن حكمه كحكم الطائر إذا كان على الغصن فلا ضمان في الأولى وضمن في الثانية، ومنها إذا أدخل شيئاً من The Book of Hajj: Chapter: If a pilgrim kills game - safe in the sanctuary, then the chick dies, and it should be that he is liable for the chick because it is from the game of the sanctuary and he caused its death if we say that preventing it from its chick is a sin. And among them: if he stood on a branch in the outer precinct while the tree's root is in the sanctuary and he shot at game in the outer precinct, or the branch was in the sanctuary and the tree and game in the outer precinct, and it should be that the one standing on the branch has the same ruling as the bird if it was on the branch, so no liability in the first case and liability in the second. And among them: if he brought in something of birds of prey and it destroyed something not by its action, and it should be that if he did not send it and it destroyed, he is liable, but if he sent it, we have previously mentioned from al-Muhit that there is no liability. And among them: if a non-muhrim sitting in the sanctuary saw game in the outer precinct, is it permissible for him to intend [to go] towards it to kill it in the outer precinct? And we have previously mentioned that game becomes [protected] by one of three [reasons]. It could be said: when it left the sanctuary, none of the [reasons] remains that would permit him [to hunt it]. And the answer is that the discussion is about the permissibility of his pursuit in the sanctuary while the purpose of pursuit is safety. And in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya and others: The extent of the sanctuary from the east side is six miles, from the second side twelve miles, from the third side eighteen miles, from the fourth side twenty-four miles. Thus said al-Faqih Abu Ja'far, and this is not known by analogy but known by transmission. Al-Sadr al-Shahid said: In what he said there is consideration, for from the second side is the miqat of 'Umra, which is al-Tan'im, and that is close to three miles. And Imam al-Nawawi mentioned in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab that its boundary from the direction of Medina is before al-Tan'im at three miles from Mecca, from the Yemen road at seven miles from Mecca, from the Ta'if road at 'Arafat from Batn Namira at seven miles, from the Iraq road at Thaniyya Jabal al-Muqta' at seven miles, from the Ji'rana road in Shi'b Abi 'Abdillah Khalid ibn 'Ali at nine miles, from the Jeddah road at ten miles from Mecca, and that there are markers erected on all its sides that Ibrahim al-Khalil set up, and Jibril would show him their locations, then the Prophet ordered their renewal, then 'Uthman, then Mu'awiya (may Allah be pleased with them), and they are still evident until now. And al-Qadi Abu al-Fadl al-Nuwayri compiled them and said: 'For the sanctuary the boundary from the land of Tayba (is) three miles, if you want to perfect it ...' Then I saw the issue explicitly stated in Matn al-Lubab: He said: 'If both die, the chick is liable, not the mother.' He said: (If we say that preventing it from its chick is a sin) in some copies: 'from the outer precinct' instead of 'from its chick', and I did not see its meaning. He only conditioned it because of what was previously mentioned: that the cause is like direct action with the condition that it be transgression. He said: (And among them: if he stood on a branch in the outer precinct...) He said in al-Nahr from al-Siraj: If the shooter was in the sanctuary and the game in the outer precinct or vice versa, it is from the game of the sanctuary. If he shot at game in the outer precinct and it fled and was hit in the sanctuary, he owes the penalty. If it was hit in the outer precinct and died in the sanctuary, it is permissible to eat it by analogy and it is makruh by istihsan. He said: (And among them: if a non-muhrim sitting in the sanctuary saw game...) He said in al-Nahr: It should not be hesitated in permissibility since there is no prohibition there. He said: (And from Yemen seven to the end: And seven miles [for] 'Iraq and Ta'if and Jeddah ten, then nine for Ji'rana, sanctuary, and from Yemen seven with its sin [letter] advanced, and it is complete, so thank your Lord for His favor.)
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the chapter concerning a pilgrim killing game. The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) expands on rulings from the original matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq). The first part deals with various scenarios: (1) If a pilgrim kills a mother bird and her chick dies as a result, the pilgrim is liable for the chick only if preventing the mother from her chick is considered a transgression. (2) If a person stands on a branch in the hal (outside Haram) but the tree's root is in the Haram, or vice versa, the liability depends on where the bird is when killed. (3) If someone brings a bird of prey into the Haram and it kills something not by his command, he is liable only if he did not send it; if he sent it, no liability according to al-Muhit. (4) If a non-muhrim sitting in the Haram sees game in the hal, may he go to kill it? The answer is yes, because the game has left the Haram and lost its protection. The commentator then shifts to a discussion of the boundaries of the Haram, citing multiple authorities and a poem by al-Nuwayri. The boundaries vary: from the east 6 miles, from the second side 12 miles, etc., but al-Sadr al-Shahid criticizes this as not matching al-Tan'im. Al-Nawawi gives different measurements from various roads. The commentator concludes by noting that the issue is known by transmission, not analogy.

Key Terms
حرم (haram): The sacred precinct around Mecca with defined boundaries.
سبب (sabab): Cause; an indirect action that leads to an effect.
معصية (ma'siya): Sin; an act of disobedience.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a muhrim brings a bird of prey into the Haram and it kills a bird without his command, why is he not liable?
A) Because the bird of prey acted on its own.
B) Because he did not send it (ursala) to kill.
C) Because the killing occurred in the Haram.
D) Because he is a muhrim and cannot be liable for animals.

Key Takeaway
Liability for game in the Haram through indirect causation requires that the cause itself be a transgression; otherwise, no penalty.

Boundaries of the Haram: Transmission vs. Analogy
Expansion on the determination of Haram boundaries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the various reported measurements of the Haram boundaries and explain why they are known only by transmission.

Core Idea
Haram boundaries are (naqli) based on reports, not analogy (qiyas).

This lesson explores a dispute among scholars about the exact distances marking the Haram boundaries. The passage emphasizes that these boundaries cannot be derived by reason but must be taken from transmitted reports (naql). We will compare the measurements given by Abu Ja'far, al-Nawawi, and others, and understand the critique by al-Sadr al-Shahid.

Guided Path
1
List the transmitted measurements
Write down the distances from each direction as reported by Abu Ja'far and then by al-Nawawi.
Miles are historical units; approximate modern equivalents are not needed.
2
Identify the basis of authority
Note that Abu Ja'far's measurements are cited as known only by transmission (naqlan), not analogy.
This means they are binding as transmitted reports.
3
Analyze al-Sadr al-Shahid's critique
He points out a contradiction: al-Tan'im (miqat for 'Umra) is only about 3 miles away, but Abu Ja'far says 12 miles on that side.
This suggests Abu Ja'far's report may be inaccurate.
4
Contrast with al-Nawawi's measurements
Al-Nawawi gives different distances from various roads, and mentions markers set by Ibrahim (AS) and renewed by later caliphs.
This version is considered more reliable and is the mufta bihi.
